<p>A <b>cephalopod</b> (pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is any member of the <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">molluscan</a> <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">class</a> <b>Cephalopoda</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> plural <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">κεφαλόποδα</span>, <i>kephalópoda</i>; "head-feet"). These exclusively marine animals are characterized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bilateral_symmetry" title="Bilateral symmetry">bilateral body symmetry</a>, a prominent head, and a set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalopod_arm" title="Cephalopod arm">arms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tentacle" title="Tentacle">tentacles</a> (<a href="/wiki/Muscular_hydrostat" title="Muscular hydrostat">muscular hydrostats</a>) modified from the primitive molluscan foot. Fishermen sometimes call them <b>inkfish</b>, referring to their common ability to squirt <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_ink" title="Cephalopod ink">ink</a>. The study of cephalopods is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Malacology" title="Malacology">malacology</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Teuthology" title="Teuthology">teuthology</a>.</p>
<p>Cephalopods became dominant during the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a> period, represented by primitive <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">nautiloids</a>. The class now contains two, only distantly related, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> subclasses: <a href="/wiki/Coleoidea" title="Coleoidea">Coleoidea</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopuses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Nautiloid" title="Nautiloid">Nautiloidea</a>, represented by <i><a href="/wiki/Nautilus_(genus)" title="Nautilus (genus)">Nautilus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Allonautilus" title="Allonautilus">Allonautilus</a></i>. In the Coleoidea, the molluscan shell has been internalized or is absent, whereas in the Nautiloidea, the external shell remains. About 800 living <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of cephalopods have been identified. Two important extinct <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Ammonoidea" title="Ammonoidea">Ammonoidea</a> (ammonites) and <a href="/wiki/Belemnoidea" title="Belemnoidea">Belemnoidea</a> (belemnites).</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:418px;max-width:418px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sepia_officinalis_Linnaeus,_1758_.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1038" data-file-width="1600" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Sepia_officinalis_Linnaeus%2C_1758_.jpg/220px-Sepia_officinalis_Linnaeus%2C_1758_.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Sepia_officinalis_Linnaeus%2C_1758_.jpg/330px-Sepia_officinalis_Linnaeus%2C_1758_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Sepia_officinalis_Linnaeus%2C_1758_.jpg/440px-Sepia_officinalis_Linnaeus%2C_1758_.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:192px;max-width:192px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benthoctopus_sp.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Benthoctopus_sp.jpg/190px-Benthoctopus_sp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Benthoctopus_sp.jpg/285px-Benthoctopus_sp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Benthoctopus_sp.jpg/380px-Benthoctopus_sp.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left:</b> A pair of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepia_officinalis" title="Sepia officinalis">Sepia officinalis</a></i> in shallow water<br/>
<b>Right:</b> <i><a href="/wiki/Benthoctopus" title="Benthoctopus">Benthoctopus</a></i> sp. on the <a href="/wiki/Davidson_Seamount" title="Davidson Seamount">Davidson Seamount</a> at 2,422 m depth</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are over 800 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_species" title="Extant species">extant</a> species of cephalopod,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> although new species continue to be described. An estimated 11,000 extinct <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a> have been described, although the soft-bodied nature of cephalopods means they are not easily fossilised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cephalopods are found in all the oceans of Earth. None of them can tolerate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a>, but the brief squid, <i><a href="/wiki/Lolliguncula_brevis" title="Lolliguncula brevis">Lolliguncula brevis</a></i>, found in <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a>, is a notable partial exception in that it tolerates <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">brackish water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Cephalopods are thought to be unable to live in freshwater due to multiple biochemical constraints, and in their +400 million year existence have never ventured into fully freshwater habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cephalopods occupy most of the depth of the ocean, from the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plain</a> to the sea surface. Their diversity is greatest near the equator (~40 species retrieved in nets at 11°N by a diversity study) and decreases towards the poles (~5 species captured at 60°N).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system_and_behavior">Nervous system and behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Nervous system and behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_intelligence" title="Cephalopod intelligence">Cephalopod intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid_giant_axon" title="Squid giant axon">squid giant axon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid_giant_synapse" title="Squid giant synapse">squid giant synapse</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalopod_aggression" title="Cephalopod aggression">cephalopod aggression</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:389px;max-width:389px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:181px;max-width:181px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oktopus_opening_a_container_with_screw_cap_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3601" data-file-width="2400" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Oktopus_opening_a_container_with_screw_cap_01.jpg/179px-Oktopus_opening_a_container_with_screw_cap_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Oktopus_opening_a_container_with_screw_cap_01.jpg/269px-Oktopus_opening_a_container_with_screw_cap_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Oktopus_opening_a_container_with_screw_cap_01.jpg/358px-Oktopus_opening_a_container_with_screw_cap_01.jpg 2x" width="179"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euprymna_scolopes_(Bobtail_squid)_behavior.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="1800" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Euprymna_scolopes_%28Bobtail_squid%29_behavior.jpg/202px-Euprymna_scolopes_%28Bobtail_squid%29_behavior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Euprymna_scolopes_%28Bobtail_squid%29_behavior.jpg/303px-Euprymna_scolopes_%28Bobtail_squid%29_behavior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Euprymna_scolopes_%28Bobtail_squid%29_behavior.jpg/404px-Euprymna_scolopes_%28Bobtail_squid%29_behavior.jpg 2x" width="202"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left:</b> An octopus opening a container with a screw cap<br/>
<b>Right:</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Bobtail_Squid" title="Hawaiian Bobtail Squid">Hawaiian bobtail squid</a>, <i>Euprymna scolopes</i>, burying itself in the sand, leaving only the eyes exposed</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cephalopods are widely regarded as the most intelligent of the <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, and have well developed senses and large <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brains</a> (larger than those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> of cephalopods is the most complex of the invertebrates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and their brain-to-body-mass ratio falls between that of <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">endothermic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">ectothermic</a> vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup> The brain is protected in a <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilaginous</a> cranium. The giant <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerve</a> fibers of the cephalopod <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)" title="Mantle (mollusc)">mantle</a> have been widely used for many years as experimental material in <a href="/wiki/Neurophysiology" title="Neurophysiology">neurophysiology</a>; their large diameter (due to lack of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myelination" title="Myelination">myelination</a>) makes them relatively easy to study compared with other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Cephalopods have also been known to climb out of their aquaria, maneuver a distance of the lab floor, enter another aquarium to feed on the crabs, and return to their own aquarium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cephalopods are social creatures; when isolated from their own kind, they will sometimes <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">shoal</a> with fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Packard1972_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Packard1972-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some cephalopods are able to fly through the air for distances of up to 50 m. While cephalopods are not particularly aerodynamic, they achieve these impressive ranges by jet-propulsion; water continues to be expelled from the funnel while the organism is in the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macia-Robinson-2004_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macia-Robinson-2004-12">[12]</a></sup> The animals spread their fins and tentacles to form wings and actively control lift force with body posture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuramatsuEtAl2013_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-MuramatsuEtAl2013-13">[13]</a></sup> One species, <i><a href="/wiki/Japanese_flying_squid" title="Japanese flying squid">Todarodes pacificus</a></i>, has been observed spreading tentacles in a flat fan shape with a mucus film between the individual tentacles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuramatsuEtAl2013_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-MuramatsuEtAl2013-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> while another, <i><a href="/wiki/Caribbean_reef_squid" title="Caribbean reef squid">Sepioteuthis sepioidea</a>,</i> has been observed putting the tentacles in a circular arrangement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Senses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cephalopods have advanced vision, can detect gravity with <a href="/wiki/Statocyst" title="Statocyst">statocysts</a>, and have a variety of chemical sense organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:34</sup> Octopuses use their arms to explore their environment and can use them for depth perception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision">Vision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_eye" title="Cephalopod eye">Cephalopod eye</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mollusc_eye" title="Mollusc eye">mollusc eye</a></div>

</div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sepia_eyelid_shape.theora.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The W-shaped pupil of the cuttlefish expanding when the lights are turned off</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most cephalopods rely on vision to detect predators and prey, and to communicate with one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serb2008_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serb2008-16">[16]</a></sup> Consequently, cephalopod vision is acute: training experiments have shown that the <a href="/wiki/Common_octopus" title="Common octopus">common octopus</a> can distinguish the brightness, size, shape, and horizontal or vertical orientation of objects. The morphological construction gives cephalopod eyes the same performance as sharks'; however, their construction differs, as cephalopods lack a cornea, and have an everted retina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serb2008_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Serb2008-16">[16]</a></sup> Cephalopods' eyes are also sensitive to the plane of <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Unlike many other cephalopods, <a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">nautiluses</a> do not have good vision; their eye structure is highly developed, but lacks a solid <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens</a>. They have a simple "<a href="/wiki/Pinhole_camera" title="Pinhole camera">pinhole</a>" eye through which water can pass. Instead of vision, the animal is thought to use <a href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfaction</a> as the primary sense for <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">foraging</a>, as well as locating or identifying potential mates.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colour_vision">Colour vision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Colour vision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cuttlefish_eye.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Cuttlefish_eye.jpg/250px-Cuttlefish_eye.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Cuttlefish_eye.jpg/375px-Cuttlefish_eye.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Cuttlefish_eye.jpg/500px-Cuttlefish_eye.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cuttlefish_eye.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A cuttlefish with W-shaped pupils which may help them discriminate colors.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Surprisingly, given their ability to change color, all octopodes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> and most cephalopods<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messenger-17_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Messenger-17-19">[19]</a></sup> are considered to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_blind" title="Color blind">color blind</a>. Coleoid cephalopods (octopus, squid, cuttlefish) have a single photoreceptor type and lack the ability to determine color by comparing detected photon intensity across multiple spectral channels. When <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflaging</a> themselves, they use their chromatophores to change brightness and pattern according to the background they see, but their ability to match the specific color of a background may come from cells such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iridophore" title="Iridophore">iridophores</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leucophore" title="Leucophore">leucophores</a> that reflect light from the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> They also produce visual pigments throughout their body, and may sense light levels directly from their body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Color_vision" title="Color vision">color vision</a> has been found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sparkling_Enope_Squid" title="Sparkling Enope Squid">sparkling enope squid</a> (<i>Watasenia scintillans</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messenger-17_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Messenger-17-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michinomae1994_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michinomae1994-22">[22]</a></sup> which achieves color vision by the use of three distinct <a href="/wiki/Retinal" title="Retinal">retinal</a> molecules (A1, sensitive to red; A2, to purple, and A4, to yellow?) which bind to its <a href="/wiki/Opsin" title="Opsin">opsin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seidou1990_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seidou1990-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, a novel mechanism for spectral discrimination in cephalopods was described. This relies on the exploitation of <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_aberration" title="Chromatic aberration">chromatic aberration</a> (wavelength-dependence of focal length). Numerical modeling shows that chromatic aberration can yield useful chromatic information through the dependence of image acuity on accommodation. The unusual off-axis slit and annular pupil shapes in cephalopods enhance this ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stubbs_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stubbs-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photoreception">Photoreception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Photoreception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, molecular evidence was published indicating that cephalopod chromatophores are photosensitive; reverse transcription polymerase chain reactions (RT-PCR) revealed <a href="/wiki/Primary_transcript" title="Primary transcript">transcripts</a> encoding <a href="/wiki/Rhodopsin" title="Rhodopsin">rhodopsin</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Retinochrome&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Retinochrome (page does not exist)">retinochrome</a> within the retinas and skin of the <a href="/wiki/Longfin_inshore_squid" title="Longfin inshore squid">longfin inshore squid</a> (<i>Doryteuthis pealeii</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Common_cuttlefish" title="Common cuttlefish">common cuttlefish</a> (<i>Sepia officinalis</i>) and broadclub cuttlefish (<i><a href="/wiki/Sepia_latimanus" title="Sepia latimanus">Sepia latimanus</a></i>). The authors claim this is the first evidence that cephalopod dermal tissues may possess the required combination of molecules to respond to light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingston2015_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kingston2015-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hearing">Hearing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hearing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some squids have been shown to detect sound using their <a href="/wiki/Statocyst" title="Statocyst">statocysts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_light">Use of light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Use of light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cuttlefish_color.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3507" data-file-width="2480" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Cuttlefish_color.jpg/220px-Cuttlefish_color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Cuttlefish_color.jpg/330px-Cuttlefish_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Cuttlefish_color.jpg/440px-Cuttlefish_color.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cuttlefish_color.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadclub_Cuttlefish" title="Broadclub Cuttlefish">broadclub cuttlefish</a> (<i>Sepia latimanus</i>) can change from camouflage tans and browns (top) to yellow with dark highlights (bottom) in less than a second.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most cephalopods possess an assemblage of skin components that interact with light. These may include <a href="/wiki/Chromatophore#Iridophores_and_leucophores" title="Chromatophore">iridophores, leucophores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromatophore" title="Chromatophore">chromatophores</a> and (in some species) <a href="/wiki/Photophore" title="Photophore">photophores</a>. Chromatophores are colored pigment cells that expand and contract in accordance to produce color and pattern which they can use in a startling array of fashions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingston2015_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kingston2015-25">[25]</a></sup> As well as providing camouflage with their background, some cephalopods bioluminesce, shining light downwards to disguise their shadows from any predators that may lurk below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a> is produced by bacterial symbionts; the host cephalopod is able to detect the light produced by these organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">Bioluminescence</a> may also be used to entice prey, and some species use colorful displays to impress mates, startle predators, or even communicate with one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup> It is not certain whether bioluminescence is actually of epithelial origin or if it is a bacterial production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encbrit_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-encbrit-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coloration">Coloration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Coloration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Animal_coloration" title="Animal coloration">Animal coloration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Animals_that_can_change_color" title="Category:Animals that can change color">Category:Animals that can change color</a></div>
<p>Cephalopods can change their colors and patterns in milliseconds, whether for <a href="/wiki/Signalling_theory" title="Signalling theory">signalling</a> (both <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">within the species</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Aposematism" title="Aposematism">warning</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Active_camouflage" title="Active camouflage">active camouflage</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup> as their chromatophores are expanded or contracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encbrit_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-encbrit-28">[28]</a></sup> Coloration is typically stronger in near-shore species than those living in the open ocean, whose functions tend to be restricted to <a href="/wiki/Camouflage#Disruptive_patterning" title="Camouflage">disruptive camouflage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup></p>
<p>Evidence of original coloration has been detected in cephalopod fossils dating as far back as the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a>; these orthoconic individuals bore concentric stripes, which are thought to have served as camouflage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Devonian cephalopods bear more complex color patterns, of unknown function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ink">Ink</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ink">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_ink" title="Cephalopod ink">Cephalopod ink</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ink_sac" title="Ink sac">ink sac</a></div>
<p>With the exception of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautilidae" title="Nautilidae">Nautilidae</a> and the species of <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a> belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">suborder</a> <a href="/wiki/Cirrina" title="Cirrina">Cirrina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> all known cephalopods have an ink sac, which can be used to expel a cloud of dark ink to confuse <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> This sac is a muscular bag which originated as an extension of the hind gut. It lies beneath the gut and opens into the anus, into which its contents – almost pure <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a> – can be squirted; its proximity to the base of the funnel means the ink can be distributed by ejected water as the cephalopod uses its jet propulsion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> The ejected cloud of melanin is usually mixed, upon expulsion, with <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a>, produced elsewhere in the mantle, and therefore forms a thick cloud, resulting in visual (and possibly chemosensory) impairment of the predator, like a <a href="/wiki/Smoke_screen" title="Smoke screen">smokescreen</a>. However, a more sophisticated behaviour has been observed, in which the cephalopod releases a cloud, with a greater mucus content, that approximately resembles the cephalopod that released it (this decoy is referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Pseudomorph#Pseudomorph_in_other_fields" title="Pseudomorph">Pseudomorph</a>). This strategy often results in the predator attacking the pseudomorph, rather than its rapidly departing prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> For more information, see <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_ink#Inking_behaviours" title="Cephalopod ink">Inking behaviors</a>.</p>
<p>The inking behaviour of cephalopods has led to a common name of "inkfish", primarily used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries">fisheries</a> science and the <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">fishing industry</a>, paralleling the terms <a href="/wiki/Whitefish_(fisheries_term)" title="Whitefish (fisheries term)">white fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oily_fish" title="Oily fish">oily fish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chtenopteryx_sicula2_-_from_Commons.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="159" data-file-width="335" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Chtenopteryx_sicula2_-_from_Commons.jpg/250px-Chtenopteryx_sicula2_-_from_Commons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b5/Chtenopteryx_sicula2_-_from_Commons.jpg 1.5x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Viscera of <i><a href="/wiki/Chtenopteryx_sicula" title="Chtenopteryx sicula">Chtenopteryx sicula</a></i></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ocythoe_tuberculata_viscera_-_from_Commons.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="500" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d7/Ocythoe_tuberculata_viscera_-_from_Commons.jpg/250px-Ocythoe_tuberculata_viscera_-_from_Commons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d7/Ocythoe_tuberculata_viscera_-_from_Commons.jpg/375px-Ocythoe_tuberculata_viscera_-_from_Commons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d7/Ocythoe_tuberculata_viscera_-_from_Commons.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Viscera of <i><a href="/wiki/Ocythoe_tuberculata" title="Ocythoe tuberculata">Ocythoe tuberculata</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulatory_system">Circulatory system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Circulatory system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cephalopods are the only mollusks with a closed circulatory system. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coleoid" title="Coleoid">Coleoids</a> have two gill <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">hearts</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Branchial_heart" title="Branchial heart">branchial hearts</a>) that move blood through the capillaries of the <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a>. A single systemic heart then pumps the oxygenated blood through the rest of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like most molluscs, cephalopods use <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a>, a copper-containing protein, rather than <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>, to transport oxygen. As a result, their blood is colorless when deoxygenated and turns blue when exposed to air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiration">Respiration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Respiration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cephalopods exchange gases with the seawater by forcing water through their gills, which are attached to the roof of the organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert1990_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert1990-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:488</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schipp1979_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schipp1979-35">[35]</a></sup> Water enters the mantle cavity on the outside of the gills, and the entrance of the mantle cavity closes. When the mantle contracts, water is forced through the gills, which lie between the mantle cavity and the funnel. The water's expulsion through the funnel can be used to power jet propulsion. The gills, which are much more efficient than those of other molluscs, are attached to the ventral surface of the mantle cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schipp1979_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schipp1979-35">[35]</a></sup> There is a trade-off with gill size regarding lifestyle. To achieve fast speeds, gills need to be small—water will be passed through them quickly when energy is needed, compensating for their small size. However, organisms which spend most of their time moving slowly along the bottom do not naturally pass much water through their cavity for locomotion; thus they have larger gills, along with complex systems to ensure that water is constantly washing through their gills, even when the organism is stationary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert1990_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert1990-34">[34]</a></sup> The water flow is controlled by contractions of the radial and circular mantle cavity muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bone1994_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bone1994-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The gills of cephalopods are supported by a skeleton of robust fibrous proteins; the lack of mucopolysaccharides distinguishes this matrix from cartilage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole2009_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole2009-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The gills are also thought to be involved in excretion, with NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> being swapped with K<sup>+</sup> from the seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schipp1979_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schipp1979-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion_and_buoyancy">Locomotion and buoyancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Locomotion and buoyancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Octopus3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2538" data-file-width="3136" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Octopus3.jpg/220px-Octopus3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Octopus3.jpg/330px-Octopus3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Octopus3.jpg/440px-Octopus3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Octopus3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Octopi swim headfirst, with arms trailing behind</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While most cephalopods can move by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jet_(gas)" title="Jet (gas)">jet</a> propulsion, this is a very energy-consuming way to travel compared to the tail propulsion used by fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12-11_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-11-39">[39]</a></sup> The efficiency of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propellor" title="Propellor">propellor</a>-driven <a href="/wiki/Jet_(fluid)" title="Jet (fluid)">waterjet</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Propulsive_efficiency#Propeller_engines" title="Propulsive efficiency">Froude efficiency</a>) is a more efficient model than <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10952883_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10952883-40">[40]</a></sup> The relative efficiency of <a href="/wiki/Jet_propulsion" title="Jet propulsion">jet propulsion</a> decreases further as animal size increases; <a href="/wiki/Paralarva" title="Paralarva">paralarvae</a> are far more efficient than juvenile and adult individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartol2008_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartol2008-41">[41]</a></sup> Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleozoic_era" title="Paleozoic era">Paleozoic era</a>, as competition with <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> produced an environment where efficient motion was crucial to survival, jet propulsion has taken a back role, with <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_fin" title="Cephalopod fin">fins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_limb" title="Cephalopod limb">tentacles</a> used to maintain a steady velocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-3">[3]</a></sup> Whilst jet propulsion is never the sole mode of locomotion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:208</sup> the stop-start motion provided by the jets continues to be useful for providing bursts of high speed - not least when capturing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prey" title="Prey">prey</a> or avoiding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-3">[3]</a></sup> Indeed, it makes cephalopods the fastest marine invertebrates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:Preface</sup> and they can out-accelerate most fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert1990_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert1990-34">[34]</a></sup> The jet is supplemented with fin motion; in the squid, the fins flap each time that a jet is released, amplifying the thrust; they are then extended between jets (presumably to avoid sinking).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartol2008_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartol2008-41">[41]</a></sup> Oxygenated water is taken into the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)" title="Mantle (mollusc)">mantle cavity</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> and through muscular contraction of this cavity, the spent water is expelled through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyponome" title="Hyponome">hyponome</a>, created by a fold in the mantle. The size difference between the posterior and anterior ends of this organ control the speed of the jet the organism can produce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shea2002_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shea2002-42">[42]</a></sup> The velocity of the organism can be accurately predicted for a given mass and morphology of animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Motion of the cephalopods is usually backward as water is forced out anteriorly through the hyponome, but direction can be controlled somewhat by pointing it in different directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Some cephalopods accompany this expulsion of water with a gunshot-like popping noise, thought to function to frighten away potential predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guerra2007_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guerra2007-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cephalopods employ a similar method of propulsion despite their increasing size (as they grow) changing the dynamics of the water in which they find themselves. Thus their paralarvae do not extensively use their fins (which are less efficient at low <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_number" title="Reynolds number">Reynolds numbers</a>) and primarily use their jets to propel themselves upwards, whereas large adult cephalopods tend to swim less efficiently and with more reliance on their fins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartol2008_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bartol2008-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nautilus_front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Nautilus_front.jpg/220px-Nautilus_front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Nautilus_front.jpg/330px-Nautilus_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Nautilus_front.jpg/440px-Nautilus_front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nautilus_front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Nautilus_belauensis" title="Nautilus belauensis">Nautilus belauensis</a></i> seen from the front, showing the opening of the hyponome</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Early cephalopods are thought to have produced jets by drawing their body into their shells, as <i>Nautilus</i> does today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells1991_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wells1991-46">[46]</a></sup> <i>Nautilus</i> is also capable of creating a jet by undulations of its funnel; this slower flow of water is more suited to the extraction of oxygen from the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells1991_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wells1991-46">[46]</a></sup> The jet velocity in <i>Nautilus</i> is much slower than in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coleoid" title="Coleoid">coleoids</a>, but less musculature and energy is involved in its production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHAMBERLAINJR1993_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHAMBERLAINJR1993-47">[47]</a></sup> Jet thrust in cephalopods is controlled primarily by the maximum diameter of the funnel orifice (or, perhaps, the average diameter of the funnel)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oDor1988_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-oDor1988-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:440</sup> and the diameter of the mantle cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oDor2000_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-oDor2000-49">[49]</a></sup> Changes in the size of the orifice are used most at intermediate velocities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oDor1988_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-oDor1988-48">[48]</a></sup> The absolute velocity achieved is limited by the cephalopod's requirement to inhale water for expulsion; this intake limits the maximum velocity to eight body-lengths per second, a speed which most cephalopods can attain after two funnel-blows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oDor1988_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-oDor1988-48">[48]</a></sup> Water refills the cavity by entering not only through the orifices, but also through the funnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oDor1988_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-oDor1988-48">[48]</a></sup> Squid can expel up to 94% of the fluid within their cavity in a single jet thrust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10952883_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10952883-40">[40]</a></sup> To accommodate the rapid changes in water intake and expulsion, the orifices are highly flexible and can change their size by a factor of twenty; the funnel radius, conversely, changes only by a factor of around 1.5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oDor1988_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-oDor1988-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some octopus species are also able to walk along the sea bed. Squids and cuttlefish can move short distances in any direction by rippling of a flap of <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> around the mantle.</p>
<p>While most cephalopods float (i.e. are <a href="/wiki/Neutral_buoyancy" title="Neutral buoyancy">neutrally buoyant</a> or nearly so; in fact most cephalopods are about 2-3% denser than seawater<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Packard1972_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Packard1972-11">[11]</a></sup>), they achieve this in different ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12-11_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-11-39">[39]</a></sup> Some, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">Nautilus</a></i>, allow gas to diffuse into the gap between the mantle and the shell; others allow purer water to ooze from their kidneys, forcing out denser salt water from the body cavity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12-11_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-11-39">[39]</a></sup> others, like some fish, accumulate oils in the liver;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12-11_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-11-39">[39]</a></sup> and some octopuses have a gelatinous body with lighter <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> replacing <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> in the body chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca12-11_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca12-11-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shell">Shell</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Shell">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cirrate_shell" title="Cirrate shell">Cirrate shell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlebone" title="Cuttlebone">Cuttlebone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gladius_(cephalopod)" title="Gladius (cephalopod)">Gladius (cephalopod)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mollusc_shell" title="Mollusc shell">Mollusc shell</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spirula_spirula.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="613" data-file-width="1500" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Spirula_spirula.jpg/220px-Spirula_spirula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Spirula_spirula.jpg/330px-Spirula_spirula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Spirula_spirula.jpg/440px-Spirula_spirula.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Cross section of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirula_spirula" title="Spirula spirula">Spirula spirula</a></i>, showing the position of the shell inside the mantle</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herklots_1859_I_2_Sepia_officinalis_-_schelp.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="240" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Herklots_1859_I_2_Sepia_officinalis_-_schelp.jpg/220px-Herklots_1859_I_2_Sepia_officinalis_-_schelp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ab/Herklots_1859_I_2_Sepia_officinalis_-_schelp.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Cuttlebone" title="Cuttlebone">Cuttlebone</a> of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepia_officinalis" title="Sepia officinalis">Sepia officinalis</a></i></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sepioteuthis_lessoniana_gladius.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="220" data-file-width="765" height="63" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Sepioteuthis_lessoniana_gladius.jpg/220px-Sepioteuthis_lessoniana_gladius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Sepioteuthis_lessoniana_gladius.jpg/330px-Sepioteuthis_lessoniana_gladius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Sepioteuthis_lessoniana_gladius.jpg/440px-Sepioteuthis_lessoniana_gladius.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Gladius of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepioteuthis_lessoniana" title="Sepioteuthis lessoniana">Sepioteuthis lessoniana</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">Nautiluses</a> are the only extant cephalopods with a true external shell. However, all molluscan shells are formed from the <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a> (outer layer of the embryo); in <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a> (<i>Sepia</i> spp.), for example, an invagination of the ectoderm forms during the embryonic period, resulting in a shell (<a href="/wiki/Cuttlebone" title="Cuttlebone">cuttlebone</a>) that is internal in the adult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baratte2007_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baratte2007-50">[50]</a></sup> The same is true of the chitinous <a href="/wiki/Gladius_(cephalopod)" title="Gladius (cephalopod)">gladius</a> of squid<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baratte2007_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baratte2007-50">[50]</a></sup> and octopuses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cirrina" title="Cirrina">Cirrate</a> octopods have <a href="/wiki/Cirrate_shell" title="Cirrate shell">arch-shaped cartilaginous fin supports</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> which are sometimes referred to as a "shell vestige" or "gladius".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Incirrina" title="Incirrina">Incirrina</a> have either a pair of rod-shaped <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stylet_(octopus)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stylet (octopus) (page does not exist)">stylets</a> or no vestige of an internal shell,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and some squid also lack a gladius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Interestingly, the shelled coleoids do not form a clade or even a paraphyletic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strugnell2007_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strugnell2007-56">[56]</a></sup> The <i>Spirula</i> shell begins as an organic structure, and is then very rapidly mineralized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Shells that are "lost" may be lost by resorption of the calcium carbonate component.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Females of the octopus genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argonauta" title="Argonauta">Argonauta</a></i> secrete a specialised paper-thin eggcase in which they reside, and this is popularly regarded as a "shell", although it is not attached to the body of the animal.</p>
<p>The largest group of shelled cephalopods, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonite" title="Ammonite">ammonites</a>, are extinct, but their shells are very common as <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a>.</p>
<p>The deposition of carbonate, leading to a mineralized shell, appears to be related to the acidity of the organic shell matrix (see <a href="/wiki/Mollusc_shell" title="Mollusc shell">Mollusc shell</a>); shell-forming cephalopods have an acidic matrix, whereas the gladius of squid has a basic matrix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:361px;max-width:361px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Logy_bay_giant_squid_1873.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1952" data-file-width="1276" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Logy_bay_giant_squid_1873.png/220px-Logy_bay_giant_squid_1873.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Logy_bay_giant_squid_1873.png/330px-Logy_bay_giant_squid_1873.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Logy_bay_giant_squid_1873.png/440px-Logy_bay_giant_squid_1873.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:135px;max-width:135px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tentacule_Abraliopsis_morisi.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="992" data-file-width="392" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Tentacule_Abraliopsis_morisi.jpg/133px-Tentacule_Abraliopsis_morisi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Tentacule_Abraliopsis_morisi.jpg/200px-Tentacule_Abraliopsis_morisi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Tentacule_Abraliopsis_morisi.jpg/266px-Tentacule_Abraliopsis_morisi.jpg 2x" width="133"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left:</b> A giant squid found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logy_Bay" title="Logy Bay">Logy Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a>, in 1873. The two long feeding tentacles are visible on the extreme left and right.<br/>
<b>Right:</b> Detail of the tentacular club of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abraliopsis_morisi" title="Abraliopsis morisi">Abraliopsis morisi</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Head_appendages">Head appendages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Head appendages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_limb" title="Cephalopod limb">Cephalopod limb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tentacle" title="Tentacle">tentacle</a></div>
<p>Cephalopods, as the name implies, have muscular appendages extending from their heads and surrounding their mouths. These are used in feeding, mobility, and even reproduction. In <a href="/wiki/Coleoidea" title="Coleoidea">coleoids</a> they number eight or ten. Decapods such as cuttlefish and squid have five pairs. The longer two, termed <i><a href="/wiki/Tentacle" title="Tentacle">tentacles</a></i>, are actively involved in capturing prey;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:225</sup> they can lengthen rapidly (in as little as 15 milliseconds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:225</sup>). In <a href="/wiki/Giant_squid" title="Giant squid">giant squid</a> they may reach a length of 8 metres. They may terminate in a broadened, sucker-coated club.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:225</sup> The shorter four pairs are termed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalopod_arm" title="Cephalopod arm">arms</a></i>, and are involved in holding and manipulating the captured organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:225</sup> They too have suckers, on the side closest to the mouth; these help to hold onto the prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:226</sup> Octopods only have four pairs of sucker-coated arms, as the name suggests, though developmental abnormalities can modify the number of arms expressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toll1991_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toll1991-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The tentacle consists of a thick central nerve cord (which must be thick to allow each sucker to be controlled independently)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> surrounded by circular and radial muscles. Because the volume of the tentacle remains constant, contracting the circular muscles decreases the radius and permits the rapid increase in length. Typically a 70% lengthening is achieved by decreasing the width by 23%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mollusca11_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-mollusca11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:227</sup> The shorter arms lack this capability.</p>
<p>The size of the tentacle is related to the size of the buccal cavity; larger, stronger tentacles can hold prey as small bites are taken from it; with more numerous, smaller tentacles, prey is swallowed whole, so the mouth cavity must be larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Externally shelled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautilidae" title="Nautilidae">nautilids</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">Nautilus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Allonautilus" title="Allonautilus">Allonautilus</a></i>) have on the order of 90 finger-like appendages, termed <i>tentacles</i>, which lack suckers but are sticky instead, and are partly retractable.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Architeuthis_beak.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1368" data-file-width="2355" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Architeuthis_beak.jpg/220px-Architeuthis_beak.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Architeuthis_beak.jpg/330px-Architeuthis_beak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Architeuthis_beak.jpg/440px-Architeuthis_beak.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Architeuthis_beak.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The two-part beak of the <a href="/wiki/Giant_squid" title="Giant squid">giant squid</a>, <i>Architeuthis</i> sp.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All living cephalopods have a two-part <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_beak" title="Cephalopod beak">beak</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:7</sup> most have a <a href="/wiki/Radula" title="Radula">radula</a>, although it is reduced in most octopus and absent altogether in <i>Spirula</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cephalopods_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-Cephalopods-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:7</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:110</sup> They feed by capturing prey with their tentacles, drawing it into their mouth and taking bites from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> They have a mixture of toxic digestive juices, some of which are manufactured by symbiotic algae, which they eject from their salivary glands onto their captured prey held in their mouth. These juices separate the flesh of their prey from the bone or shell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> The salivary gland has a small tooth at its end which can be poked into an organism to digest it from within.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The digestive gland itself is rather short.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> It has four elements, with food passing through the crop, stomach and caecum before entering the intestine. Most digestion, as well as the absorption of nutrients, occurs in the digestive gland, sometimes called the liver. Nutrients and waste materials are exchanged between the gut and the digestive gland through a pair of connections linking the gland to the junction of the stomach and caecum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> Cells in the digestive gland directly release pigmented excretory chemicals into the lumen of the gut, which are then bound with mucus passed through the anus as long dark strings, ejected with the aid of exhaled water from the funnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyle_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-Boyle-18">[18]</a></sup> Cephalopods tend to concentrate ingested heavy metals in their body tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radula">Radula</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Radula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Radula#In_cephalopods" title="Radula">Radula § In cephalopods</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1917" data-file-width="2567" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg/220px-Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg/330px-Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg/440px-Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Veined_Octopus_-_Amphioctopus_Marginatus_eating_a_Crab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Amphioctopus_marginatus" title="Amphioctopus marginatus">Amphioctopus marginatus</a></i> eating a crab</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The cephalopod radula consists of multiple symmetrical rows of up to nine teeth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> – thirteen in fossil classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1995_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1995-66">[66]</a></sup> The organ is reduced or even vestigial in certain octopus species and is absent in <i><a href="/wiki/Spirula" title="Spirula">Spirula</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1995_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1995-66">[66]</a></sup> The teeth may be homodont (i.e. similar in form across a row), heterodont (otherwise), or ctenodont (comb-like).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1995_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1995-66">[66]</a></sup> Their height, width and number of cusps is variable between species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1995_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1995-66">[66]</a></sup> The pattern of teeth repeats, but each row may not be identical to the last; in the octopus, for instance, the sequence repeats every five rows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1995_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1995-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:79</sup></p>
<p>Cephalopod radulae are known from fossil deposits dating back to the Ordovician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbott1999_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbott1999-67">[67]</a></sup> They are usually preserved within the cephalopod's body chamber, commonly in conjunction with the mandibles; but this need not always be the case;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> many radulae are preserved in a range of settings in the Mason Creek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Radulae are usually difficult to detect, even when they are preserved in fossils, as the rock must weather and crack in exactly the right fashion to expose them; for instance, radulae have only been found in nine of the 43 ammonite genera,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kruta2011_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kruta2011-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and they are rarer still in non-ammonoid forms: only three pre-Mesozoic species possess one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbott1999_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbott1999-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Excretory_system">Excretory system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Excretory system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most cephalopods possess a single pair of large <a href="/wiki/Nephridium" title="Nephridium">nephridia</a>. Filtered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogenous_waste" title="Nitrogenous waste">nitrogenous waste</a> is produced in the <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardial</a> cavity of the <a href="/wiki/Branchial_heart" title="Branchial heart">branchial hearts</a>, each of which is connected to a nephridium by a narrow canal. The canal delivers the excreta to a bladder-like renal sac, and also resorbs excess water from the filtrate. Several outgrowths of the lateral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vena_cava" title="Vena cava">vena cava</a> project into the renal sac, continuously inflating and deflating as the branchial hearts beat. This action helps to pump the secreted waste into the sacs, to be released into the mantle cavity through a pore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IZ_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-IZ-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Nautilus</i>, unusually, possesses four nephridia, none of which are connected to the pericardial cavities.</p>
<p>The incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> is important for shell formation in terrestrial molluscs and other non-molluscan lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loest1979_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loest1979-72">[72]</a></sup> Because <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> (i.e. flesh) is a major constituent of the cephalopod diet, large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> are produced as waste. The main organs involved with the release of this excess ammonium are the gills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boucher-Rodoni1994_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boucher-Rodoni1994-73">[73]</a></sup> The rate of release is lowest in the shelled cephalopods <i><a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">Nautilus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sepia_(genus)" title="Sepia (genus)">Sepia</a></i> as a result of their using <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> to fill their shells with gas to increase buoyancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boucher-Rodoni1994_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boucher-Rodoni1994-73">[73]</a></sup> Other cephalopods use ammonium in a similar way, storing the <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> (as <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a>) to reduce their overall density and increase buoyancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boucher-Rodoni1994_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boucher-Rodoni1994-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_life_cycle">Reproduction and life cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cephalopod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reproduction and life cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="mw-stack" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:right;">
<div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright">
